  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4020ad63-9f85-4f74-b7ee-de3a88f41fb7'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9372962060045379122"><i>Beatles</i> er en munter og vemodig roman om fire Beatles-frelste Oslogutter av årgang 1951, fra de som syvendeklassinger står på spranget inn i voksenverdenen - "Vi tok framtida på forskudd, og den så sabla bra ut" - til 25. september 1972. Ingen går tørskodd når dønningene fra ungdomsopprøret ute i Europa skyller inn over dem og deres beskyttende tilværelse. Aftenleserne har</span>
  <span id="freeText9372962060045379122" style="display:none"><i>Beatles</i> er en munter og vemodig roman om fire Beatles-frelste Oslogutter av årgang 1951, fra de som syvendeklassinger står på spranget inn i voksenverdenen - "Vi tok framtida på forskudd, og den så sabla bra ut" - til 25. september 1972. Ingen går tørskodd når dønningene fra ungdomsopprøret ute i Europa skyller inn over dem og deres beskyttende tilværelse. Aftenleserne har talt: Ingen etterkrigsroman beskriver Oslo bedre enn Lars Saabye Christensens oppvekstskildring fra Frogner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9372962060045379122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_181626438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181626438" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181626438?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788202093051" href="/book/show/9970841-beatles">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hamsun fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Manny by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4094158-flaneurette">Flaneurette</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=older-women-younger-men">older-women-younger-men</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=well-i-think-its-funny">well-i-think-its-funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=translation-is-impossible">translation-is-impossible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts">too-sexy-for-maiden-aunts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=swedish-norwegian-and-danish">swedish-norwegian-and-danish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181626438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15456108188584597749"><br/><b>You know it ain't easy</b><br/><br/>What is this book about? I can see why most of the reviewers are having trouble. It's got laugh-out-loud slapstick, heartbreaking tragedy, unforgettable characters, ridiculous stereotypes, breathtaking lyricism, flat ultrarealist prose... you see the problem. But one thing's clear. There's a whole lot about the Beatles.<br/><br/><b>So let me introduce to you</b><br/><br/>Kim Karlsen is 11 when <i>Love me do</i> comes out. He and his three friends are immediate converts, and identify with John, Paul, George </span>
  <span id="freeText15456108188584597749" style="display:none"><br /><b>You know it ain't easy</b><br /><br />What is this book about? I can see why most of the reviewers are having trouble. It's got laugh-out-loud slapstick, heartbreaking tragedy, unforgettable characters, ridiculous stereotypes, breathtaking lyricism, flat ultrarealist prose... you see the problem. But one thing's clear. There's a whole lot about the Beatles.<br /><br /><b>So let me introduce to you</b><br /><br />Kim Karlsen is 11 when <i>Love me do</i> comes out. He and his three friends are immediate converts, and identify with John, Paul, George and Ringo. (He's Paul). They see the world through Beatles-shaped glasses. The novel traces their varying fortunes from 1965 to 1972.<br /><br /><b>Norwegian wood</b><br /><br />Like Jan Kjærstad, who's clearly paying homage to Christensen at times, the book is both quintessentially Norwegian and completely universal. Kim is Everyman - to be exact, Everyfuckedupteen - but he also happens to be an Everyman who lives in a specific part of Oslo and assumes you have an intimate knowledge of its geography, history and social structure. You know it makes sense. I mean, look at Dante, right?<br /><br /><b>Living is easy with eyes closed</b><br /><br />Did I say fucked up? I had forgotten quite how fucked up people were in the 60s. So unbelievably blind to what was going on around them, to the most obvious and elementary facts of life. But, at the same time, occasionally so in touch with the most profound things. <br /><br /><b>Carrying pictures of Chairman Mao</b><br /><br />The left-wing politics. Comrades, I warn you that this part is toe-curlingly embarrassing, but he just delivers it with a straight face. You actually can't tell whether he's being ironic or not. An impressive piece of historical reconstruction.<br /><br /><b>Man, you've been a naughty girl</b><br /><br />Love makes you stupid. Kim's in love with both Nina and Cecilie and he can't decide between them. I guess that makes him twice as stupid as most people? Or maybe he's just more upfront about it.<br /><br /><b>Very strange</b><br /><br />There's magical realism too.<br /><br /><b>All the lonely people</b><br /><br />Life is hard. Terrible things happen all the time, when we least expect them. The book is full of helpless, lost people: Dragen, Fred, Uncle Hubert, Jørgen, Kim himself. If you like Hamsun, Christensen is surely his greatest and most faithful disciple.<br /><br /><b>These are words that go together well</b><br /><br />He has a wonderful feeling for the poetry of everyday language. It's like the Beatles (duh) or Huck Finn.<br /><br /><b>Yeah, yeah, yeah</b><br /><br />I know, I say this too often, but the book is untranslatable. Looking at the other reviews, nearly all the Norwegians love it and nearly all the foreigners wonder what the fuss is about. Turning it around, would you expect <i>Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds</i> to be any good in Norwegian? But if you have even a passing acquaintance with the language (my own Norwegian isn't great), consider putting it on your list.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15456108188584597749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1076872206" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1076872206" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1076872206?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906413071" href="/book/show/6124983-beatles">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1076872206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3854260799823176245">Wavering between <b>3.5</b> and <b>4</b> ...<br/><br/>Notice the absence of the <i>The</i> in the title: there are The Beatles and then there are Gunnar, Kim, Seb and Ola -- four good-hearted, The Beatles-loving teenagers growing up in Oslo in the mid-60s. But what might seem like a mischievous comic romp of a novel turns deadly serious as politics, police brutality and drugs become a part of the boys' lives into the very early 70s.<br/><br/>I loved the concept and greatly enjoyed the Oslo setting (as I'd recently been there); but what</span>
  <span id="freeText3854260799823176245" style="display:none">Wavering between <b>3.5</b> and <b>4</b> ...<br /><br />Notice the absence of the <i>The</i> in the title: there are The Beatles and then there are Gunnar, Kim, Seb and Ola -- four good-hearted, The Beatles-loving teenagers growing up in Oslo in the mid-60s. But what might seem like a mischievous comic romp of a novel turns deadly serious as politics, police brutality and drugs become a part of the boys' lives into the very early 70s.<br /><br />I loved the concept and greatly enjoyed the Oslo setting (as I'd recently been there); but what bothered me, along with a couple of pet peeves near the end, was the length. For what it is -- a coming-of-age story -- it's kind of long and somewhat repetitive. (Such wordiness being the only issue I remember having with LSC's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/125523.The_Half_Brother" title="The Half Brother by Lars Saabye Christensen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Half Brother</a>.)<br /><br />Remember in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2187.Middlesex" title="Middlesex by Jeffrey Eugenides" rel="nofollow noopener">Middlesex</a> (a novel I loved) when Cal flees to San Francisco and you think: <i>What? Why have we left Detroit? Isn't it about time to wrap up this book?</i> Well, that's what it feels like when three of the lads hitchhike to Paris to rescue George, excuse me, Seb. When Kim (Paul) uses his study loan to impulsively buy a flight to Iceland, I felt like I did watching the movie <i>Titanic</i> when Rose jumped out of the lifeboat and I screamed to myself: <i>Wait. What? This movie's not almost over!?!</i> ... Never mind, it isn't nearly as bad as that.<br /><br />LSC is a good writer and, especially in the beginning, I loved the insights of his narrator, Kim. A parallel between Munch's <i>The Scream</i> (the one in Oslo's National Gallery) and a photograph from Vietnam that Kim recalls while viewing the painting is impressive, even more so as it continues as an extended metaphor. LSC plots well, drawing you into wondering why Kim is in the family summer house in autumn and what in the world happened to him to get him there. When you find out, it feels right.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3854260799823176245" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26416859-calzean?shelf=author-norway">author-norway</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer1102407680741933608">Four boys growing up in Oslo from 1965 to 1972. They collect Beatles records first in awe of their brilliance then slowly more critical as their tastes and preference in music widen. <br/>The narrator is Kim. He is quick with a quip and tells too many liars. He is a drifter and is not all that stable. His friends include a stutterer, a spiritualist and a protector. They play soccer and gradually find girlfriends. They smoke a bit which for some of them turn into hard drugs, they drink a lot. All come</span>
  <span id="freeText1102407680741933608" style="display:none">Four boys growing up in Oslo from 1965 to 1972. They collect Beatles records first in awe of their brilliance then slowly more critical as their tastes and preference in music widen. <br />The narrator is Kim. He is quick with a quip and tells too many liars. He is a drifter and is not all that stable. His friends include a stutterer, a spiritualist and a protector. They play soccer and gradually find girlfriends. They smoke a bit which for some of them turn into hard drugs, they drink a lot. All come from a family of working class men.<br />The backdrop is the music scene, long hair, Vietnam War, street protests, street gangs, and a feeling of drifting. There is a strange back robbery at Kim's fathers bank, the death of a friend, abortion, and a lot of other storylines which could be read as metaphors and caricatures of the time.<br />At the end of the book Kim has a nervous breakdown and escapes to find his on and off first girlfriend Nina. It is a book that resonates of the revolutionary times.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_30433755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30433755" />
    <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi"><img alt="Lorenzo Berardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417557095p2/183865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30433755?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorenzo Berardi" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi">Lorenzo Berardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781906413071" href="/book/show/6124983-beatles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=a-my-english-library">a-my-english-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=norwegian">norwegian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30433755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer994307571682732372">Reading 'Beatles' was another long walk I took down Memory Lane.<br/>Bless Lars Saabye Christensen for setting another novel in that specific area of Oslo I remember so fondly! <br/><br/>The English edition I owe boasts that 'Beatles' is 'The International Bestseller' and in fact this is the book that made Mr Christensen famous in Norway and abroad. <br/>Not to mention that a few months ago I spotted a hoodie eagerly leafing through this same book at a bus stop in the sleepy English town of Hereford (just don't ask</span>
  <span id="freeText994307571682732372" style="display:none">Reading 'Beatles' was another long walk I took down Memory Lane.<br />Bless Lars Saabye Christensen for setting another novel in that specific area of Oslo I remember so fondly! <br /><br />The English edition I owe boasts that 'Beatles' is 'The International Bestseller' and in fact this is the book that made Mr Christensen famous in Norway and abroad. <br />Not to mention that a few months ago I spotted a hoodie eagerly leafing through this same book at a bus stop in the sleepy English town of Hereford (just don't ask me how I ended up there!). Actually this single readerspotting would be enough to confirm that 'Beatles' did indeed become a bestseller. I guess the title helped, though.<br /><br />Published in 1984, when its author was only 33, this novel has been translated into 16 languages, sold hundreds of thousands of copies and - surprise surprise! - is going to become a major Norwegian movie that will have its premiere on February 2014. Apparently the chief reason why it took so long to bring 'Beatles' onto the big screen is that the prerequisite to have the movie made was to ensure that the Fab Four songs would have been in it. And it tooks ages (and money) to get that.<br /><br />Putting its International Bestseller reputation aside, as I wrote above, 'Beatles' is one of those books having a very personal meaning to me. <br />Just like it happened with 'The Half Brother' - the first novel by Christensen that I read - most of the action here is set in a two mile radius from Majorstua, one of the main intersections in West Oslo. Call me weird, but spotting toponyms such as Blindern, Bygdøy Allé, Solli Plass, Slemdalsveien, Chateau Neuf and Uranienborg Park made me actually happier than the countless references to The Beatles themselves.<br /><br />With a plot taking place between 1965 and 1972 and with every chapter titled after a Beatles' song (plus a couple of McCartney and Lennon solo career singles), Christensen wrote a wistful and clever novel.<br />The four protagonists - Kim, Gunnar, Seb and Ola - idolize the Fab Four to the point they identify themselves with them thus becoming Paul, John, George and Ringo. <br />Even though their own dream of making a band called The Snafus is perpetually postponed due to the lack of music instruments, the four Oslo kids grow up listening in almost religious awe to each and every Beatles LP and EP. As it happens, their tastes in music do evolve over the course of the years leading them to 'discover' Bob Dylan, The Doors, The Mothers of Invention, and the blues. But the Beatles stay untouchable and every rumour implying that the Fab Four are on their way to split up is returned to sender by the boys in disbelief. <br /><br />Aged only 14 at the beginning of the novel, Kim, Gunnar, Seb and Ola are 21 at its end. As you might wonder, not only their favourite records have changed but also their passions and interests switching from football, skiing and fishing to girls, alcohol, drugs and politics. That's why topics such as the Vietnam War, marijuana planting, the involvement in the ranks of the Young Socialists and the Norwegian European Communities membership referendum in 1972 take the floor.<br /><br />Gradually what had begun as the story of four easy and semi-idyllic childhoods turn into a gloomy and disillusioned tale with the odd funny moment. One by one all of the four boys fail at some stage of their young lives. Some of them fall deep into an abyss of either drug addiction, alcoholic stupour or nervous breakdown but somehow manage to come up for air, at least for a while. Just like The Beatles themselves, if you like. <br /><br />And it's with this bleak atmosphere that the novel ends up. <br />I know that Christensen wrote two sequels but it looks like they have not yet been translated into English.<br />All in all I'm not entirely sure I'd like to read the sequels. On the one hand I prefer to leave Kim, Gunnar, Seb and Ola where they are, at the young age of 21. On the other hand I remember too well the disappointment I felt when reading 'The Closed Circle' by Jonathan Coe whose excellent 'The Rotters' Club' bears many a similitude with 'Beatles' (four teenagers, the 1960s turning into the 1970s, music, politics, petting). <br /><br />If these translations see the light, I hope that a better translator than Don Bartlett will be given the job. Nothing personal, Don, but it's the second time that your work doesn't convince me at all after what you did to 'Child Wonder' by Roy Jacobsen. <br />Tacky mistakes aside (in the English edition Kim comes back to Iceland and tells his crosswords maniac dad he was in a 'cold place, six letters' as the seven lettered 'Iceland' is spelled 'Island' - six letters - in Norwegian), Mr Bartlett here seems to enjoy leaving the reader in the dark. The examples of this sadistic pleasure of the translator are countless, but I will mention a couple which give you a general idea of what I mean.<br /><br />Page 503. The years is 1972. Kim gets a university loan. Don meekly translates: 'Four Ibsens and the basic grant'. Any idea of what that means? You need to Google 'Ibsen banknote 1970' to find out. Which is four 1,000 Norwegian crowns (kroner) banknotes with the face of Henrik Ibsen on them.<br />Page 493. Kim is in Iceland visiting a former girlfriend of his. First-person narrative. All in a sudden in the middle of a dialogue, Don switches to the third-person narrative ('she told him'). <br /><br />And then there is the issue with Norwegian addresses and cultural references. To translate them or not to translate them? - Mr Bartlett might have pondered. The problem is that he didn't make his mind up.<br />So it happens that the magazine 'Nå' ('Now') and the newspaper 'Aftenposten' ('The Evening Post') keep their Norwegian names while the leftist newspaper 'Klassekampen' becomes 'Class Conflict' and the public television NRK becomes the 'Norwegian Broadcasting Corporation'. I wonder why.<br />Another problem comes up with the translation of nouns. Now Norwegian (both Bokmål and Nynorsk) adds up suffixes so that, say, 'Storting' (the National Parliament) becomes 'det Stortinget'. But the name of the Parliament is Storting and not Storting-et. Now, go and tell this to Don Bartlett for whom it's 'the Stortinget'.<br /><br />I am sorry, I am really sorry to spoil my review by taking the piss out of the translator but I believe that 'Beatles' would have deserved a better treatment. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="994307571682732372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText6377322844349387804" style="display:none">I remember seeing this book in my home since 2005, which the date of release of the portuguese translation. I've just decided to read it this summer, in late June, at the breaks of some study for the university exams. Sometimes, it was hard to stop reading to sleep a bit, and sometimes, without noticing it, it was already 4 or 5 o'clock in the morning. Now about the book, it was great, and one of the best things I have ever read in my life. A good and very well told story, moving in some parts, dramatic and romantic, hipster and almost 'beat' in many parts of the text, together with full detail of its time, the sixties, the Vietnam War, the cultural revolution, the invasion of Czechoslovakia, May 68 in France. And of course, music: The Beatles, The Stones, The Doors, The Animals, bands that I also love.<br />I loved reading it, and possibly some years from now, I will need to read it again, and try to feel once again the pleasure I have got in this last days. This has to be 5 stars :)</span>

Christensen's career was spawned at the same time The Big Chill and The Wonder Years were foisted upon the world. He's not a bad </span>
  <span id="freeText14657093672543274555" style="display:none">This is largely a book I know because of its being namechecked by (and clearly an inspiration to) Karl Ove "Fuck You and Fuck My Dad" Knausgaard. While it is likewise is a deep investigation into the life of an ordinary Norwegian kid, it's more overtly (at least for this Yank reader) an extended exercise in boomer nostalgia, something I find almost uniquely repulsive. Christensen's career was spawned at the same time The Big Chill and The Wonder Years were foisted upon the world. He's not a bad writer, but I really, really don't need this.</span>

<div id="review_2197600126" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2197600126" />
    <a title="Murray" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64620666-murray"><img alt="Murray" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513470893p2/64620666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2197600126?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murray" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murray" href="/user/show/64620666-murray">Murray</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2197600126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1997471571542151141">Nobody writes with such originality about the shift from boyhood to adolescence and beyond as this author. I loved young Kim’s description of the theatre as ‘worse than church and gymnasium combined’ as he endured an outing with his mother. Down the road a few years and Kim and his friends travel back home from Paris ‘through a stinking Europe that lay on our skin like grey dirt’. For me, the most beautiful part of the book is the encounter that the boys have with Fred’s mother at the same time </span>
  <span id="freeText1997471571542151141" style="display:none">Nobody writes with such originality about the shift from boyhood to adolescence and beyond as this author. I loved young Kim’s description of the theatre as ‘worse than church and gymnasium combined’ as he endured an outing with his mother. Down the road a few years and Kim and his friends travel back home from Paris ‘through a stinking Europe that lay on our skin like grey dirt’. For me, the most beautiful part of the book is the encounter that the boys have with Fred’s mother at the same time as they are taking their confirmation lessons. Stunning writing. This book doesn’t have the same wild, tortured, thrilling style as ‘the half brother’ (one of my favourite books), but that’s not what he’s aiming for here - I thoroughly enjoyed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1997471571542151141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2467675579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2467675579" />
    <a title="Michael Armijo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4488615-michael-armijo"><img alt="Michael Armijo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564082059p2/4488615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2467675579?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Armijo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Armijo" href="/user/show/4488615-michael-armijo">Michael Armijo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2467675579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14294947368474804218">Back in 2014 I had the opportunity to see the Norwegian film that was based on this book at the PALM SPRINGS INTERNATIONAL FILM FESTIVAL. I loved it so much that I found a copy of the book (in ENGLISH--not easy to find) as it was an International Best-Seller in Europe. I finally took the time to read it. I loved how each chapter was titled after a BEATLES song and the growing-up trials and tribulations (some humorous and some challenging) of four young boys in the Oslo area named KIM, OLA, SEB a</span>
  <span id="freeText14294947368474804218" style="display:none">Back in 2014 I had the opportunity to see the Norwegian film that was based on this book at the PALM SPRINGS INTERNATIONAL FILM FESTIVAL. I loved it so much that I found a copy of the book (in ENGLISH--not easy to find) as it was an International Best-Seller in Europe. I finally took the time to read it. I loved how each chapter was titled after a BEATLES song and the growing-up trials and tribulations (some humorous and some challenging) of four young boys in the Oslo area named KIM, OLA, SEB and GUNNAR. Each one believed they were one of the BEATLES. They wanted to be 'them'. Kim was PAUL, the storyteller. OLA was RINGO. Seb was JOHN. Gunnar was GEORGE.<br />I had forgotten the story and recalled by the books' end why I loved it so much. <br /><br />LIFE is a journey. That's what it's about. Here are the lines that captured me (without spoiling too much):<br /><br />Chapter: I FEEL FINE<br />I dreamt about Ringo, John, George and Paul. I dreamt that I was one of them, that I was Paul McCartney, that I had his round, sorrowful eyes that all the girls screamed themselves have to death over. I sat up in bed, wide awake. But I am one of them, I thought aloud, and laughed. I am one of THE BEATLES. …Even lying has limits. You can lie up to a certain point and make it sound good. After that it is just insane.<br />I like to call him THE WHALE because whales sing, too, they sing because they are lonely and the oceans are much too large for them.<br />Now everything went as smooth as butter.<br />You will soon come to realize that PUNCTUALITY is one of the cornerstones of the adult world.<br />“It doesn’t matter who answers if the answer is the same, does it?” I said, almost stunned by my own logic.<br />I don’t really know what it was with Uncle, there were these knots inside his head that would not loosen and at times they were tighter than at others, and on this Sunday they were unusually rigid.<br />We reap our greatest knowledge through trial and error. <br />“Singing is about letting yourself go,” he concluded. “Letting yourself go and having control at the same time. You must have control. But don’t be afraid of your voice. Everyone has a big voice inside them. Here!” He thumped his chest.<br /><br />Chapter: RUBBER SOUL<br />We had a sneak preview of the future and it looked damn good.<br />“I fancy some cocoa,” she said on a sudden whim. “Do you?”<br /><br />Chapter: PAPERBACK WRITER<br />I pressed my nose against the glass. Strange to be in a completely new place. Tingle of excitement. Pigeons. Hot dog stalls. Black bikes. I rolled down the window. Bakery smells. Freshly baked cakes and bread. I leaned back against the seat, closed my eyes and felt happy. Happy and completely calm.<br /><br />Chapter: YELLOW SUBMARINE<br />The water lay before us, shiny and still, and warm air caressed our bodies.<br /><br />Chapter: STRAWBERRY FIELDS FOREVER<br />Living is easy with eyes closed.<br />WHAT IT MEANS TO BE COURAGEOUS was the title and I was very pleased, I had written five pages about how it wasn’t possible to be courageous unless you were frightened first.<br />“Pre-Party’s under way. I can see.” <br />We nodded. Pre-Party. That’s what it was.<br />“I’m bored,” he said. “Do you get bored often? I’m almost always bored. That’s why I’m going to be an actor. So that I can be anyone I feel like. And then I’ll be spared the boredom.”<br />“What’s an eunuch?”<br />“Life’s slave,” he said. “Deprived of his virility and left with his desire intact. Sometimes it’s unbearable.”<br /><br />Chapter: A DAY IN THE LIFE<br />Just before the bell rang for the next lesson he came over to us at the drinking fountain and told us that snakes sleep for a whole month after eating a sheep. And the snake was the Devil’s Beast.<br />I wrote about SPACE TRAVEL. I wrote about humans that are so tiny and space that is so immense and I got something in about a door that has to be opened to enter the blue space. I was in the groove. If there was not enough room for us on earth, we could settle on other planets.<br />I thought about the snake skin, that the future was like a snake, a boa constrictor which dropped from the trees, and that we had already been swallowed, zero chance of escaping, we were already in the warm belly of the future and were being digested.<br />Everything had gone TOPSY-TURVY. And yet everything was as it should be. <br />Then I got to know…the music. And then I had to change, too, to let the music flow inside me like water, to open myself completely like a door that had been jammed shut for a long time, that is the only way I can express it. Like raising yourself…A DAY IN THE LIFE. A day like this, of which there is only one.<br />Kept thinking, kept thinking, that everything had taken such a long time and yet it had gone so damned fast.<br /><br />Chapter: HELLO GODBYE<br />I lay in bed and was suddenly afraid of the fear, the fear that came too late.<br /><br />Chapter: REVOLUTION<br />My footsteps echoed on the stone floors. Far away, beneath me, I heard thee music throbbing like a frog’s heart and I danced my solitary dance.<br />“A nation must be allowed to determine its own fate without outside interference.”<br />“Do you think long hair’s attractive?”<br />“Yes, especially under the arms.”<br />“When you write you’ve got to lie, too,” he said.<br />“Yes,” I said. “Of course, but the secret is to lie will.”<br />“Well you would know. You’re the best liar of us all.”<br />TH SOUND OF SILENCE: That made me feel calm and happy. That was how life should be.<br />“We always know more than we think we know,” said the detective.<br /><br />Chapter: CARRY THAT WEIGHT<br />“I’m not some plaything!” I shrieked. “You can’t just get me to do whatever takes your whim! Do you understand?”<br />The conversation over dessert died a death.<br />“What are you going to do after your final exams?” the mother asked.<br />“Not sure yet,” I answered in a clear voice. “Maybe study languages.”<br />“That’s what’s wrong with you young people,” the father broke in, blowing out all the candles in one breath. “You can’t see AHEAD. You don’t’ have PERSPECTIVES! I started off with two empty hands!”<br /><br />WHILE FISHING ---<br />“The hardest part comes now, you know,” he chatted. “Anyone can get a bite, but not everyone can get it up.”<br />He pulled carefully, let the line run.<br />“First you have to get to know it, gauge its strength, see through its tricks.”<br />He let the fish go. It didn’t swim out but across.<br />“NO TWO ARE THE SAME, you see. They have their methods, all of them. Some go straight to the bottom, others come up, some go along with you until you reckon they’re home and dry and then tear themselves free at the last moment. But one thing they have in common. It’s always a fight. Isn’t that right? Always a fight.”<br />I liked the regular sound of water falling behind me. I ought to write a letter, wouldn’t have to be that long. Could write something about fountains. A sparrow hopped round my feet and was not in the slightest bit intimidated. Anyway, I wasn’t dangerous.<br /><br />LIVE IN THE NOW---<br />“It’s gone quickly, in fact, “I said.<br />“What has?”<br />“Time.” <br />“Time doesn’t go,” he said. “Time just IS. Watches are for materialists and social climbers.”<br />He showed me his thin bare wrist.<br />“It’s all about living now,” he said. “No point reminiscing. No point planning. LIFE’s about NOW.”<br /><br />“The THEATER is the truth,” he exclaimed in all seriousness. “Isn’t it? We’re acting all the time, with each other.. It makes us like. We fool each other and pretend not to notice. But on the stage everyone is clear about their role. Only on the stage are we truly honest.”<br />He had a goal. I was just going in circles. I was a carousel round the mirror, insomnia, the record player and fear.<br />My eyelids stung. I could no longer see. My head fell forward and I leaned my forehead against his chest. He settled his hand against my neck and held me tight. Then I felt his cheek against mine, his mouth, I put my arm around him, we were in the middle of the storm ad I cried on his breast.<br />I went out into the May night thinking about all the things that were in the past now.<br /><br />Chapter: MY SWEET LORD<br />“We have to have something to hold onto,” he went on. “Everyone has to have a fixed point, a light, a meaning…Otherwise we’re empty shells and life is a wasted second.”<br /><br />Chapter: WILD LIFE<br />The New Year was around the corner. It was a time for resolutions. I had none. I hadn’t done anything wrong. <br /><br />Chapter: LOVE ME DO<br />“LIFE doesn’t always turn out the way you imagine. You’ll have to realize that someday, too.”<br />Get away. Before it is too late.<br />The clouds in the sky cleared and we threw ourselves into the mayhem, we were carried away, delirious with happiness.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14294947368474804218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1242653923" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1242653923" />
    <a title="Valetta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4528302-valetta"><img alt="Valetta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415268348p2/4528302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1242653923?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Valetta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Valetta" href="/user/show/4528302-valetta">Valetta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788865641330" href="/book/show/25261108-beatles">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1242653923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10494316112312298283">This is a really problematic book to talk about, after almost 600 pages I still can't decide if and how much I actually liked it.<br/>I definitely got bored in several occasions, mainly because of the abundance of repetitive situations (a trait common to many Scandinavian narrators, apparently) but I was also enthralled, moved and touched more often than not.<br/>What hooked me in the beginning was the title: I grew up with The Beatles, just like the Kim, Gunnar, Seb and Ola, but without "the revolution" </span>
  <span id="freeText10494316112312298283" style="display:none">This is a really problematic book to talk about, after almost 600 pages I still can't decide if and how much I actually liked it.<br />I definitely got bored in several occasions, mainly because of the abundance of repetitive situations (a trait common to many Scandinavian narrators, apparently) but I was also enthralled, moved and touched more often than not.<br />What hooked me in the beginning was the title: I grew up with The Beatles, just like the Kim, Gunnar, Seb and Ola, but without "the revolution" since it were the nineties, not the sixties (I was an old-fashioned teenager) so a coming-of-age story punctuated by the Fab <br />Four's songs was literally music to my hears.<br /><br />I was expecting a light recount of teenage romps molded by The Beatles' lyric but this is only partly true: the story of four almost idyllic childhoods evolves in a gloomy tale of disillusionment just like the idealistic expectations of the sixties are crushed by drugs and materialism, just like the carefree English boys who sang "Love me do" develop a political conscience and a penchant for oriental meditation techniques.<br /><br />The four protagonists religiously follow The Beatles' production, from the enthusiasm of the mid-sixties to the desperation of the definitive split: the songs' lyrics are not a reflection of their own lives but they are their fixed point, the only constant in the ever flowing river of time. Even when they start to grow apart, even when the songs are not so convincing, even when more mature song-writers like Bob Dylan make their appearance, a new Beatles' LP can connect them, <br />symbol of a friendship that survives at the core despite years of separations and the inevitable conflicts that adult life brings.<br /><br />This must have seemed a nostalgic book already in the 80s, when it was first published and the expectations of the previous decade had just been disillusioned and it sounds even more so now, when those <br />revolutionary years seem like a sad bed time story.<br />In some ways, this is a sad bedtime story, a tale of four adolescent that struggle to find a place in the adult world but have no real goal (and adults don't really help with that), which leads to some spectacular failures. <br /><br />Similarly, this novel doesn't seem to have a real purpose but it appears like a sequence of ordinary events, punctuated by tragedies that somehow manage to remain marginal, until it comes at full circle, just like Kim's first person narration. But despite this, it also feels incredibly real and, if sometimes it sound stereotyped, it is only because it is only too faithful to the age and times it wants to remember: the reactionary parents, the empty political abstractions, the alcohol, the drugs, the class divisions and, above all, the teenage selfishness and obliviousness that one of the protagonists seems incapable of letting go, proving in the end to be the most fragile of the four, when he seemed the stronger.<br /><br />This is both the limit and the key strength of the novel, its repetitiveness somehow redeemed by Christensen powerful narrative style, for once beautifully rendered by the Italian translation. I often find Scandinavian authors to have flats and monotonous writing styles but Christensen voice shows a lyricism and warmth really compelling, while cleverly scattering hints on the true nature of the story throughout the book and achieving a sort of "magical realism" atmosphere both mesmerizing and perplexing that almost compensate some banalities of the plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10494316112312298283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2178028613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2178028613" />
    <a title="Halvor (Raknes)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49780289-halvor-raknes"><img alt="Halvor (Raknes)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464721957p2/49780289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2178028613?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Halvor (Raknes)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Halvor (Raknes)" href="/user/show/49780289-halvor-raknes">Halvor (Raknes)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788257402440" href="/book/show/36558642-beatles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49780289-halvor-raknes?shelf=norw-fiction-1950-1999">norw-fiction-1950-1999</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2178028613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11943833682969034296">Why didn't I like this book which so many people feel captures so well the spirit of a generation?<br/><br/>I think this novel has a very "cheap" approach to its story. I have only read one other (much shorter) book by this author, so I don't know if this reflects his writing abilities or if it is a calculated way of "bottom trawling", i.e. using the lowest common denominator approach to catch the widest audience possible. To me the result becomes unacceptable. The characters <b>completely</b> lack any psycholog</span>
  <span id="freeText11943833682969034296" style="display:none">Why didn't I like this book which so many people feel captures so well the spirit of a generation?<br /><br />I think this novel has a very "cheap" approach to its story. I have only read one other (much shorter) book by this author, so I don't know if this reflects his writing abilities or if it is a calculated way of "bottom trawling", i.e. using the lowest common denominator approach to catch the widest audience possible. To me the result becomes unacceptable. The characters <b>completely</b> lack any psychological make-up, i.e. they are two-dimensional at best. The merest allusions to background issues suffice to explain why one (two) become drug addicts, and concerning the main character, his habitual lying in any and all situations to avoid explaining anything real has no explanation whatsoever. One can expect such a person to be confronted with some major stumbling blocks along the way, and Kim Karlsen indeed fails in getting to grips with life and ends up with a psychosis in a psychiatric ward. But this isn't really explained. The connections are very opaque.<br /><br />The first 2/3 of the book is straightforward coming-of-age narrative with little drama. The latter third richly compensates for this though with lots of drama, but nothing appears to me to be natural and organic but rather thought out in an author's imagination. And, it is an author with perspectives on life that do not seem to stray from the experiences, notions and expectations of the average person. He has none of the hallmarks of a judge of character or of possessing any bit of wisdom. This of course would seem to be a good recipe for gathering a huge following of easy-to-please readers wanting entertainment rather than emotional or intellectual challenge. But it doesn't make for great literature.<br /><br />There were also some rather incredulous "jump the shark" incidents as well as several that were merely very far-fetched.<br /><br />To me, the value of having read this book lies entirely in getting acquainted with the Norwegian canon. Which is the same reason I have for reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/755594.Margit_Sandemo" title="Margit Sandemo" rel="nofollow noopener">Margit Sandemo</a>. However, as Saabye Christensen has managed to stay on top of his game for more than forty years, I shall probably read more of his books. The good thing is they are very easy reads.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11943833682969034296" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1356558913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1356558913" />
    <a title="Tanja Berg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg"><img alt="Tanja Berg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558953813p2/5935491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1356558913?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanja Berg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanja Berg" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg">Tanja Berg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788202093051" href="/book/show/9970841-beatles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=scandinavian">scandinavian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5935491-tanja-berg?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1356558913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15988369697057951629">Rating 3.4* out of 5. This is not a story of the Beatles, but of four friends that are fans, growing up in Oslo in the 1960's. It's a fairly average coming of age tale, there isn't really anything new. Friendship, love, school, death, revolution. The Oslo/Norway angle is probably important for locals, it didn't add anything in itself to me. I enjoyed reading this, but felt like I was missing out on cues - which I probably was.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6871464632904051040" style="display:none">I like books narrated by a male, I've always been fond of them because male characters talk less and think more so you can actually know what is inside their head more than with a female character (which is usually very confused in terms of thoughts). Kim, in these terms, let you go into his life, his fears, his childish ambitions, his solitude and for so many aspects of his attitude towards the world I deeply felt what was inside his head. The end, though, was very disappointing. I have to admit that I wish it would never end, but I'm curious of reading the following books!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7836090985183224640">"(...) her eyes were dead, there was something she had seen which had reduced them to ashes."<br/><br/>Saabye Christensen can bloody write, I'll give him that. Unfortunately, for me anyway, he lacks the ability to create an engaging plot, and his promising coming-of-age tale set in late-sixties Oslo tanks somewhere in the middle of <i>realskole</i> and never really recovers.<br/><br/>I thoroughly enjoyed half of this book, and I honestly think that he could've finished at the 300 page mark and had produced a decent novel.</span>
  <span id="freeText7836090985183224640" style="display:none">"(...) her eyes were dead, there was something she had seen which had reduced them to ashes."<br /><br />Saabye Christensen can bloody write, I'll give him that. Unfortunately, for me anyway, he lacks the ability to create an engaging plot, and his promising coming-of-age tale set in late-sixties Oslo tanks somewhere in the middle of <i>realskole</i> and never really recovers.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed half of this book, and I honestly think that he could've finished at the 300 page mark and had produced a decent novel. Alas, he fell into the trap of so many others (looking at you GRRM) and just didn't know when to move away from the typewriter. <br /><br />Still, his prose is excellent, so if you're into that sort of thing give the first 300 pages a go - I promise you won't be let down.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LoO21LvBpM/q5WHYSsPuvD1Qy/WceWQx0JC1ZRA1znjR3ww6SXPlrGFxOpA/o7ZZxyPgqHD8vGRBDrO339tkdg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Det er historien om å velge noe annet enn det som forventes av en, men samtidig bare ønske seg et vanlig liv.\n\n«Jeg vil ikke at livet skal stå stille, og det skjer mye i livet mitt også, selv om jeg ikke har født et nytt menneske. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13149176411962442324">Lars Saabye Christensen is a gifted storyteller, a narrator who is imaginative, but equally down to earth. His realism alternates between poetic image and ingenious incident, conveyed in supple metropolitan language and slang that never smacks of the artificial or forced. His heroes possess a good deal of self-irony. Indeed, critics have drawn parallels with the black humour of Woody Allen. But be</span>
  <span id="freeText13149176411962442324" style="display:none">Lars Saabye Christensen is a gifted storyteller, a narrator who is imaginative, but equally down to earth. His realism alternates between poetic image and ingenious incident, conveyed in supple metropolitan language and slang that never smacks of the artificial or forced. His heroes possess a good deal of self-irony. Indeed, critics have drawn parallels with the black humour of Woody Allen. But beneath the liveliness of his portrayal melancholy always lurks in the books.<br />Since his début in 1976 Saabye Christensen has written ten collections of poetry, five collections of short stories and twelve novels. His great break through came with the novel <i>Beatles</i> in 1984. The book store sale of over 200,000 copies of the Norwegian edition has made this one of the greatest commercial successes in Norway, and it was voted the best novel of the last 25 years by Dagbladet's readers in 2006.</span>
